<div id="review_3174256824" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3174256824" />
    <a title="Piyangie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie"><img alt="Piyangie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590579939p2/48056505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3174256824?book_show_action=true">Jun 15, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Piyangie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Piyangie" href="/user/show/48056505-piyangie">Piyangie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/13512082-the-hunchback-of-notre-dame">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=my-library">my-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48056505-piyangie?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3174256824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1073601836121546947">I'm now quite resigned to being disappointed in books that are written by my favourite classical authors. It looks like I'm in the process of discovering at least one book by each of them to my dislike. <i> The Hunchback of Notre-Dame</i> is Victor Hugo's contribution to that lot. But it is still disheartening when a book you liked as a teen becomes a total nightmare as an adult. Perhaps it is not fair for me to draw a comparison like that, for I understand now that what I've read and liked as a teen w</span>
  <span id="freeText1073601836121546947" style="display:none">I'm now quite resigned to being disappointed in books that are written by my favourite classical authors. It looks like I'm in the process of discovering at least one book by each of them to my dislike. <i> The Hunchback of Notre-Dame</i> is Victor Hugo's contribution to that lot. But it is still disheartening when a book you liked as a teen becomes a total nightmare as an adult. Perhaps it is not fair for me to draw a comparison like that, for I understand now that what I've read and liked as a teen was an abridged version of the book. And of course, I loved the Disney movie too. But the complete unabridged book is unlike either of them. <br /><br />I have many grievances against this book; the first is the length. This story could have been told in half of the words that he had used. There were too detailed descriptions of the Notre-Dame architecture and the medieval society and politics in general. It was important for Hugo to describe medieval society and politics and the general architecture of Notre-Dame, but it needn't be painfully detailed. A succinct description would have been ample to serve the purpose. The irrelevant and excessive details made the story secondary, and the story proper didn't begin until half the book is gone. <br /><br />The second is the tone of Hugo's writing. It didn't suit the tragic side of the story. There should be some gravity to carry the idea of tragedy in a tragic story, but instead, the writing was light and impassive for the most part. There was also satire which I thought most unsuited. The whole tone of the book made me devoid of emotion. I couldn't pity the innocent persecuted Esmeralda nor Quasimodo, a victim of a different sort. If I felt anything, it was only the utter loathing for the antagonist, Dom Frollo, and anger toward Pheobus whom I also considered as an antagonist. <br /><br />The third was my dissatisfaction with the flow of the story which was time and again disrupted by Hugo's love for description and explanations. Because of this, the pace of the story was quite disturbed. Likewise was my reading experience. Instead of being taken on a smooth straight road, I was taken on a rough road with many a bend in which I jostled this way and that way till my head was swimming. It was tiring and to be quite honest I had to resort to a chapter or so of another book just to feel alright. <br /><br />This doesn't mean that the book was flawed. I still liked the story. My venting here surely rises from my being unable to enjoy it as I wished. I felt Hugo has robbed my pleasure with his Dickensian style of verbosity and satire, and his impassive tone. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1073601836121546947" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Years ago I used to take out audiobooks on CD from my local library and listen to them when I was driving, but then car manufacturers stopped putting CD players in cars, and for various reasons I didnâ€™t get into the habit of listening to audiobooks via downloads, until now.   <span id="freeText14523822843928879607" style="display:none">Itâ€™s quite a few years since I last listened to an audio book. Years ago I used to take out audiobooks on CD from my local library and listen to them when I was driving, but then car manufacturers stopped putting CD players in cars, and for various reasons I didnâ€™t get into the habit of listening to audiobooks via downloads, until now. Thereâ€™s a moderate but limited selection available from same library, so I decided to start with Victor Hugoâ€™s meandering but majestic novel, which Iâ€™d never before read. I listened to this in English translation, and I daresay something of the book may have been lost in that. <br /><br />I suspect that if I had tried to read this rather than listen to it, I might have given up in the early stages, since the opening section featuring Pierre Gringoireâ€™s play seemed rambling and initially directionless. Moreover, I hadnâ€™t realised that Victor Hugo had an interest in architecture that bordered on the obsessional. Entire chapters of the book are taken up with the architectural history of Notre Dame de Paris, the architecture of old Paris, and the role of architecture in culture. I found the last of these was a bit of a struggle to get through.<br /><br />But I was won over by the story, which is magnificent. With four thousand reviews on this site at the time of writing, Iâ€™m not going to spend too much time on adding comments. The complex and interconnected stories of the main characters; the memorable characters themselves; the fantastic descriptions of medieval Paris; the two stories of romantic obsession; the associated story of controlling sexual obsession; and the sense of inexorable fate within the novel; altogether make it easy to see why this has become one of the most famous tales of all time. Stick with it, and it all comes together!<br /></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer508839062" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9827299218722787196">I want to state clearly that the book is not bad. I gave it two stars, and by that I mean it <b>is</b> OK! First of all, there are some really gorgeous lines. Secondly, Hugo has the ability to put you in the middle of an event; you are right there and it is whirling around you. Thirdly, he has a talent for weaving non-fictional facts into a fictional story. <br/><br/>Sometimes the writing feels wordy. Other times, Hugo expresses himself superbly, beautifully, elegantly.<br/><br/>The fictional elements are what give me the</span>
  <span id="freeText9827299218722787196" style="display:none">I want to state clearly that the book is not bad. I gave it two stars, and by that I mean it <b>is</b> OK! First of all, there are some really gorgeous lines. Secondly, Hugo has the ability to put you in the middle of an event; you are right there and it is whirling around you. Thirdly, he has a talent for weaving non-fictional facts into a fictional story. <br /><br />Sometimes the writing feels wordy. Other times, Hugo expresses himself superbly, beautifully, elegantly.<br /><br />The fictional elements are what give me the most trouble. Hugo and his writing and particularly <b>this</b> book belong to the French Romantic Movement of literature. It is not merely that romance is a central theme, but rather that the whole style of writing is romantic, sentimental, exaggerated and overwrought. Excess and exaggeration are the defining ingredients of every event that occurs. Personally, I can deal with romance if presented in a realistic, believable manner. That is not what is delivered here! None of the fictional elements of this story can be characterized as down to earth, believable or realistic. <br /><br />At the start, you work on understanding what is going on. The confusion clears, and the reader begins to know who is who and what is happening. The momentum builds. Most is still within bounds. Then, all breaks loose and reality totally dissolves. Some excitement is fine. I was engaged; I thought the events were exciting. Then, with one thing on top of the other, all credibility is lost. This draws for you how it feels to progress through the book.<br /><br />There are long chapters devoid of characters or plot. In these the time frame departs from the Middle Ages, goes centuries back and up to when the book was printed in 1831. Hugo leaves the story and focuses on architecture, its essence, its importance and various styles. He speaks of the history of Paris, that is to say how the city of Paris has developed over centuries. These sections are interesting, but they are weakly tied to the story. Hugo is delivering a message. As a result of the book, in Paris, buildings of the Gothic style came to be restored! This being exactly what the author had attempted to achieve. <br /><br />There is humor, often ironic in tone, criticizing the Church, royalty, aristocracy and class. Pay attention to the lines, there is humor everywhere. Individual characters are poked at and made fun of.<br /><br />This is a book of historical fiction, written by Hugo primarily from September 1830 to February 1831, but it is about events occurring during three months in 1482. The setting is Paris under the reign of King Louis XI. The cathedral itself is a central character. In French, the book was originally entitled <u>Notre-Dame de Paris</u>. The English translation by Frederic Shoberl in 1833 changed the title to <u>The Hunchback of Notre-Dame</u> to attract readers of Gothic fiction popular in Britain at that time.<br /> <br />This is a book about head-over-heels, passionate love, love at first sight. Love without bounds. It is about unrequited love. It is also about love, not of the heart, but of convenience. It is about being torn between love of a woman and love of God, faith and religion. <br /><br />There is witchery, murder, attempted rape, hangings and torture. Each occurring several times.<br /><br />I listened to the audiobook narrated by Bill Homewood. I donâ€™t think he does a bad job. The way he reads captures the feel of the authorâ€™s words. Nevertheless, the evil characters sound the same--mean and gruff and dark. There is the angelic Esmeralda, sounding meek. Contrary to this, the other women are shrill. You can group the characters into types; listening you know to which group each individual belongs. If you are really lazy, you need not even listen to the words, just listen to the tones! I say this as a joke, as a poke. In my view, it is the author who has over-simplified his characters; the narrator is only mirroring what the author has created.<br /><br />I prefer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13661.Victor_Hugo" title="Victor Hugo" rel="nofollow noopener">Victor Hugo</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24280.Les_Mis_rables" title="Les MisÃ©rables by Victor Hugo" rel="nofollow noopener">Les MisÃ©rables</a>. It isnâ€™t as fanciful as this and has more interesting historical tidbits. Both have lyrical lines, both have exciting episodes, and both enable a reader to experience another time and place in history. I like how Victor Hugo writes, but the story of <u>The Hunchback of Notre-Dame</u> is too unbelievable for me!<br /><br />It is interesting to note that in France the authorâ€™s poetry collections are more popular than his novels.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9827299218722787196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9711794392278506112">This is definitely my favourite classic, I loved the dark atmosphere of the book, and the setting being Notre-Dame, which is one of my favourite places in Paris. It's not entirely dark and horrible, there are times when it can be humorous, but it's brutal in terms of showing what obsession can become when it's misunderstood as love and the true form of love shown by other characters. <br/><br/>When starting this book, it was really difficult to get into which is why I had to force myself to keep reading u</span>
  <span id="freeText9711794392278506112" style="display:none">This is definitely my favourite classic, I loved the dark atmosphere of the book, and the setting being Notre-Dame, which is one of my favourite places in Paris. It's not entirely dark and horrible, there are times when it can be humorous, but it's brutal in terms of showing what obsession can become when it's misunderstood as love and the true form of love shown by other characters. <br /><br />When starting this book, it was really difficult to get into which is why I had to force myself to keep reading until about 60 pages in (with the introduction) when I got into the plot. The story follows different characters, as well as flashbacks and Hugo's knowledge of Notre-Dame and Paris as a city. I also love how the cathedral is a metaphor for beauty and the beast as a balanced wholeâ€” the gargoyles being a representation of Quasimodo and the famous Rose window being Esmeralda, 2 distinct features of the cathedral. <br /><i>*spoilers ahead*</i><br />I didn't like Frollo at all, I found him to be a fucking creeper and I didn't sympathize at all with him, and I'm baffled as how people could love him. the stuff he did was sickening:â€” the attempted rape of Esmeralda, forcing her to love him, laughing when she was hanged, not even caring for his adopted son, Quasimodo, when he was being jeered by the crowd. He was nasty and I didn't feel for him at all, because he didn't deserve to be sympathized with. <br /><br />Esmeralda, as much as I liked her for being compassionate towards Quasimodo, was shallow and naive. I could have played a drinking game as to how many times she says Phoebus' name during her scenes. She was foolish and pathetically in love with Phoebus because he was handsome when really he was cold and lecherous and only wanted to get in her pants. Like Frollo, she thought that lust was love, when really it was not. <br /><br />The story was heart-breaking and tragic, it really showed how appearances and social positions in society can be deceiving, and how cruel it could be for individuals who were good. <br /><br />Overall rating is <b>4.5</b> </span>

Nevertheless, he manages to render a dark environment joyous, almost funny at times. <br/><br/>For me, the protagonist is not Quasimodo but Esmeralda. Itâ€™s her inner and outer struggles we have access to the most. As usual, Victor Hugo portrayed them realistically, neither all good nor all bad, thus humane and credible. Even Gringoire and Claude are portrayed that way, as human beings subjected to primal instincts and humane spiritu</span>
  <span id="freeText938810705823020719" style="display:none">Victor Hugoâ€™s first real novel, written under duress from his publisher!! Nevertheless, he manages to render a dark environment joyous, almost funny at times. <br /><br />For me, the protagonist is not Quasimodo but Esmeralda. Itâ€™s her inner and outer struggles we have access to the most. As usual, Victor Hugo portrayed them realistically, neither all good nor all bad, thus humane and credible. Even Gringoire and Claude are portrayed that way, as human beings subjected to primal instincts and humane spirituality.<br /><br />In this novel Victor Hugo attacks Prejudice and Justice simultaneously: the deaf judge, judging a deaf defendant; and the judgment of Esmeraldaâ€™s goat!!!<br /><br />Quasimodoâ€™s inner conflict over Claude at the end is deeply moving. Claude brought him up as father, then broke his heart by trying to harm Esmeralda. Ultimately, Quasimodoâ€™s spirituality wins over his animal-like devotion to Claude...<br /><br />The final scene where we discover the skeleton of Quasimodo next to Esmeralda brought me to tears.<br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14720854247653794038">Was this a just fairy tale? I mean a darker fairy tale, where the frog didnâ€™t get kissed, and instead ran away with the goat? (Thatâ€™s a little of a spoiler). No, seriously, Hugo put a lot of time into researching this Paris of 1482, and this Cathedral, but he held off writing it until time became desperate, this according to Andre Maurois in the afterword. Then, he spent weeks locked in a room, wearing a head-to-toe sweater that he couldnâ€™t leave the house in, and crunched out the actual text. S</span>
  <span id="freeText14720854247653794038" style="display:none">Was this a just fairy tale? I mean a darker fairy tale, where the frog didnâ€™t get kissed, and instead ran away with the goat? (Thatâ€™s a little of a spoiler). No, seriously, Hugo put a lot of time into researching this Paris of 1482, and this Cathedral, but he held off writing it until time became desperate, this according to Andre Maurois in the afterword. Then, he spent weeks locked in a room, wearing a head-to-toe sweater that he couldnâ€™t leave the house in, and crunched out the actual text. So, there is a lot of Hugoâ€™s outsized ego here, in all its, well, charm maybe. Actually he had a lot of fun with the text, which is full of expansive humor, long-winded ideas only Hugo appreciate, and a complete panoramic of Paris from the top of the Cathedral, recreated from his research and likely freely filled-in with details he didnâ€™t know or wanted; in any case, the medieval city and this building are both very much there. <br><br>I read this with a group on Litsy over 8 weeks, in separate short burst with lots of in-app discussion between. The timing was in response to the tragic fire this past May. It really felt light read for such a famous classic. But then these characters are embedded in my head. I now feel I know Quasimodo and his keeper, the dark sorcerer archdeacon Claude Frollo of the Notre-Dame cathedral; Esmeralda and her anti-hero, the penniless philosopher Pierre Gringoire, and few other characters too, particularly Esmeralda's remarkable little goat, Djali.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562820352i/27808301.jpg" width="250" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>My take is that this feels like eastern fairy tale, something maybe out of 1001 Nights, that was transported to medieval Paris, and then wrapped in light philosophizing lessons of sorts on love, beauty, corruption, mobs, fate, bad kings and fatally flawed presumed-guilty-driven legal systems. (Here justice isn't just blind, but also deaf.) Perhaps he might have had a recent failed revolution in mind, and a disheartening restoration. Maurois thinks it's a dark work and certainly the end is darker than a reader who doesn't know might expect. But the humor and fun touches are mostly what I have mind when I think about it.<br><br>While I did enjoy reading and discussing this, the book never really wowed me. I gave it 3.5 stars (rounded down here on GR) <br><br>-----------------------------------------------<br><br>30. <b>The Hunchback of Notre Dame by Victor Hugo</b><br><b>published</b>: 1831<br><b>translated</b> 1965 from French by Walter J. Cobb<br><b>afterword</b> 1965 by Andre Maurois, translated from French by Phyllis La Farge<br><b>format</b>: 502-page Signet Classic paperback<br><b>acquired</b>: from my childhood<br><b>read</b>: May 5 â€“ Jun 29<br><b>time reading</b>: 21 hr 28 min, 2.6 min/page<br><b>rating</b>: 3Â½<br><br>-----------------------------------------------<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1562820352i/27808302._SX540_.png" width="900" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Paris sometime before the fire. (The image is from an article on the fire with some kind of cool panoramic visuals, here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.abc.net.au/news/2019-04-18/notre-dame-walk-through/11024512">https://www.abc.net.au/news/2019-04-1...</a> )</span>

<div id="review_2711967380" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2711967380" />
    <a title="Antoinette" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5328952-antoinette"><img alt="Antoinette" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503842818p2/5328952.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_639929465" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/639929465" />
    <a title="Wanda" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda"><img alt="Wanda" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528903039p2/9246169.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/639929465?book_show_action=true">Jun 12, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wanda" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wanda" href="/user/show/9246169-wanda">Wanda</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780679642572" href="/book/show/398206.The_Hunchback_of_Notre_Dame">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=z-1001-books">z-1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=classics">classics</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer639929465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7106870606131987163">I donâ€™t know about you, but I think about obsessional crimes and stalking as modern phenomena, exacerbated by life in huge cities. The Hunchback of Notre-Dame demonstrates that there is truly nothing new under the sun. Victor Hugo wrote this tale of obsession in the 1800s. The gypsy girl, La Esmeralda, has the misfortune of attracting the obsessional gaze of two men, the archdeacon Claude Frollo and his protÃ©gÃ©, the deformed bell-ringer of the cathedral, Quasimodo. She, in her turn, is fixated o</span>
  <span id="freeText7106870606131987163" style="display:none">I donâ€™t know about you, but I think about obsessional crimes and stalking as modern phenomena, exacerbated by life in huge cities. The Hunchback of Notre-Dame demonstrates that there is truly nothing new under the sun. Victor Hugo wrote this tale of obsession in the 1800s. The gypsy girl, La Esmeralda, has the misfortune of attracting the obsessional gaze of two men, the archdeacon Claude Frollo and his protÃ©gÃ©, the deformed bell-ringer of the cathedral, Quasimodo. She, in her turn, is fixated on handsome Captain Phoebus, who couldnâ€™t care less about her although he is willing to take advantage of her when an opportunity presents itself.<br /><br />None of these people actually know one anotherâ€”they have only observed from afar and projected their own fantasies onto other people. Quasimodo has the most reason for his adoration of La Esmeraldaâ€”she brought him water while he was incapacitated at the pillory during an undeserved punishment. Earlier, we see La Esmeralda save Pierre Gringoire, the unsuccessful playwright, from hanging by accepting him as a temporary husband. Pierre is somewhat disappointed when he discovers that she intends a platonic relationship, but is sensible enough to appreciate that her kindness has spared his life. <br /><br />La Esmeralda is presented as a kind, good person. But like many women, she finds herself the focus of unwanted male attention. We often think of stalking in relation to celebrity, but in reality many ordinary citizens find themselves the object of obsession of other â€œregularâ€ people. A waitress may, by serving a cup of coffee, unwittingly launch an obsessive on a mission to â€œownâ€ her. Having had a small brush with such behaviour myself, I have realized how startlingly easy it is to become involved in such situations. There are so many lonely people living in our cities, who are used to being ignored while resenting it. If your job requires you to be polite and helpful, these folks may misinterpret your intentions. The crumbs of attention that they receive from you may trigger that hunger for more, beginning something that you never meant to start and which you feel powerless to stop.<br /><br />At the same time, La Esmeralda is guilty of a similar behaviourâ€”she knows nothing about Phoebus except that he is handsome and wears a beautiful uniform. She is very young and it is like a young woman today becoming enamoured of a celebrity. Unlike many, La Esmeralda has the opportunity to meet her crush and is only prevented from consummating her desires by her stalker, Archdeacon Frollo. <br /> <br />None of this can end well. Modern instances of stalking are liable to end in death, either of the pursuer or the pursued. The HoND deals with these apparently timeless topicsâ€”Iâ€™m reminded of Shakespeareâ€™s tragedies, especially Othello. Victor Hugoâ€™s tale definitely deserves its reputation as classic literature.<br /></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('5qhW6MHMqGmFKSQsrme48muo+QDRR5xGTIOGnjt4s1Hcjj86epJhASICkE1ZkrItvfceyB/KEiEX/cAv9jtByw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }
